class="jsx-1464850800 preface"><div class="jsx-1464850800 author"><div class="jsx-566588255 Author"><a href="https://www.datacamp.com/profile/Sicelo" target="_blank" class="jsx-566588255"><div style="background-image:url(https://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/fetch/t_avatar_thumbnail/https://cdn.datacamp.com/community/assets/placeholder_avatar-7f673b5d40e159404a56b5931250cc73.png);border-radius:20px;min-width:40px;min-height:40px" class="jsx-3208234818 Avatar"></div><div class="jsx-566588255 info"><div class="jsx-566588255 name">Sicelo Masango</div><div class="jsx-566588255 date"><span>July 26th, 2018</span></div></div></a></div></div><div class="jsx-1464850800 tags"><div class="jsx-2792531181 TagLine"><div class="jsx-1764811326 Tag"><span class="jsx-1764811326 title">python</span></div></div></div><h1 class="jsx-1464850800 pageTitle">Web Scraping using Python</h1><div class="jsx-1464850800 description pageDescription">In this tutorial, you'll learn how to extract data from the web, manipulate and clean data using Python's Pandas library, and data visualize using Python's Matplotlib library.</div></div><div class="markdown"><div><p>Web scraping is a term used to describe the use of a program or algorithm to extract and process large amounts of data from the web. Whether you are a data scientist, engineer, or anybody who analyzes large amounts of datasets, the ability to scrape data from the web is a useful skill to have. Let's say you find data from the web, and there is no direct way to download it, web scraping using Python is a skill you can use to extract the data into a useful form that can be imported.</p>
<p>In this tutorial, you will learn about the following:</p>
<p>â¢    Data extraction from the web using Python's Beautiful Soup module</p>
<p>â¢    Data manipulation and cleaning using Python's Pandas library</p>
<p>â¢    Data visualization using Python's Matplotlib library</p>
<p>The dataset used in this tutorial was taken from a 10K race that took place in Hillsboro, OR on June 2017. Specifically, you will analyze the performance of the 10K runners and answer questions such as:</p>
<p>â¢    What was the average finish time for the runners?</p>
<p>â¢    Did the runners' finish times follow a normal distribution?</p>
<p>â¢    Were there any performance differences between males and females of various age groups?</p>
<h1 id="web-scraping-using-beautiful-soup">Web Scraping using Beautiful Soup</h1>
<p>Using Jupyter Notebook, you should start by importing the necessary modules (pandas, numpy, matplotlib.pyplot, seaborn). If you don't have Jupyter Notebook installed, I recommend installing it using the Anaconda Python distribution which is available on the internet. To easily display the plots, make sure to include the line %matplotlib inline as shown below.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> pandas <span class="hljs-keyword">as</span> pd
<span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> numpy <span class="hljs-keyword">as</span> np
<span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> matplotlib.pyplot <span class="hljs-keyword">as</span> plt
<span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> seaborn <span class="hljs-keyword">as</span> sns
%matplotlib inline
</code></pre>
<p>To perform web scraping, you should also import the libraries shown below. The urllib.request module is used to open URLs. The Beautiful Soup package is used to extract data from html files. The Beautiful Soup library's name is bs4 which stands for Beautiful Soup, version 4.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-keyword">from</span> urllib.request <span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> urlopen
<span class="hljs-keyword">from</span> bs4 <span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> BeautifulSoup
</code></pre>
<p>After importing necessary modules, you should specify the URL containing the dataset and pass it to urlopen() to get the html of the page.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">url = <span class="hljs-string">"http://www.hubertiming.com/results/2017GPTR10K"</span>
html = urlopen(url)
</code></pre>
<p>Getting the html of the page is just the first step. Next step is to create a Beautiful Soup object from the html. This is done by passing the html to the BeautifulSoup() function. The Beautiful Soup package is used to parse the html, that is, take the raw html text and break it into Python objects. The second argument 'lxml' is the html parser whose details you do not need to worry about at this point.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">soup = BeautifulSoup(html, <span class="hljs-string">'lxml'</span>)
type(soup)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>bs4.BeautifulSoup
</code></pre><p>The soup object allows you to extract interesting information about the website you're scraping such as getting the title of the page as shown below.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-comment"># Get the title</span>
title = soup.title
print(title)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>&lt;title&gt;2017 Intel Great Place to Run 10K \ Urban Clash Games Race Results&lt;/title&gt;
</code></pre><p>You can also get the text of the webpage and quickly print it out to check if it is what you expect.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-comment"># Print out the text</span>
text = soup.get_text()
<span class="hljs-comment">#print(soup.text)</span>
</code></pre>
<p>You can view the html of the webpage by right-clicking anywhere on the webpage and selecting "Inspect." This is what the result looks like.</p>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Picture1_w0u783.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>You can use the find_all() method of soup to extract useful html tags within a webpage. Examples of useful tags include &lt; a &gt; for hyperlinks, &lt; table &gt; for tables, &lt; tr &gt; for table rows, &lt; th &gt; for table headers, and &lt; td &gt; for table cells. The code below shows how to extract all the hyperlinks within the webpage.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">soup.find_all(<span class="hljs-string">'a'</span>)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>[&lt;a class="btn btn-primary btn-lg" href="/results/2017GPTR" role="button"&gt;5K&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="http://hubertiming.com/"&gt;Huber Timing Home&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="#individual"&gt;Individual Results&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="#team"&gt;Team Results&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="mailto:timing@hubertiming.com"&gt;timing@hubertiming.com&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="#tabs-1" style="font-size: 18px"&gt;Results&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a name="individual"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a name="team"&gt;&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="http://www.hubertiming.com/"&gt;&lt;img height="65" src="/sites/all/themes/hubertiming/images/clockWithFinishSign_small.png" width="50"/&gt;Huber Timing&lt;/a&gt;,
 &lt;a href="http://facebook.com/hubertiming/"&gt;&lt;img src="/results/FB-f-Logo__blue_50.png"/&gt;&lt;/a&gt;]
</code></pre><p>As you can see from the output above, html tags sometimes come with attributes such as class, src, etc. These attributes provide additional information about html elements. You can use a for loop and the get('"href") method to extract and print out only hyperlinks.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">all_links = soup.find_all(<span class="hljs-string">"a"</span>)
<span class="hljs-keyword">for</span> link <span class="hljs-keyword">in</span> all_links:
    print(link.get(<span class="hljs-string">"href"</span>))
</code></pre>
<pre><code>/results/2017GPTR
http://hubertiming.com/
#individual
#team
mailto:timing@hubertiming.com
#tabs-1
None
None
http://www.hubertiming.com/
http://facebook.com/hubertiming/
</code></pre><p>To print out table rows only, pass the 'tr' argument in soup.find_all().</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-comment"># Print the first 10 rows for sanity check</span>
rows = soup.find_all(<span class="hljs-string">'tr'</span>)
print(rows[:<span class="hljs-number">10</span>])
</code></pre>
<pre><code>[&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td&gt;Finishers:&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;577&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;, &lt;tr&gt;&lt;td&gt;Male:&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;414&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;, &lt;tr&gt;&lt;td&gt;Female:&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td&gt;163&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;, &lt;tr class="header"&gt;

&lt;td&gt;&lt;/td&gt;
&lt;/tr&gt;]
</code></pre><p>The goal of this tutorial is to take a table from a webpage and convert it into a dataframe for easier manipulation using Python. To get there, you should get all table rows in list form first and then convert that list into a dataframe. Below is a for loop that iterates through table rows and prints out the cells of the rows.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-keyword">for</span> row <span class="hljs-keyword">in</span> rows:
    row_td = row.find_all(<span class="hljs-string">'td'</span>)
print(row_td)
type(row_td)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>[&lt;td&gt;14TH&lt;/td&gt;, &lt;td&gt;INTEL TEAM M&lt;/td&gt;, &lt;td&gt;04:43:23&lt;/td&gt;, &lt;td&gt;00:58:59 - DANIELLE CASILLAS&lt;/td&gt;, &lt;td&gt;01:02:06 - RAMYA MERUVA&lt;/td&gt;, &lt;td&gt;01:17:06 - PALLAVI J SHINDE&lt;/td&gt;, &lt;td&gt;01:25:11 - NALINI MURARI&lt;/td&gt;]





bs4.element.ResultSet
</code></pre><p>The output above shows that each row is printed with html tags embedded in each row. This is not what you want. You can use remove the html tags using Beautiful Soup or regular expressions.</p>
<p>The easiest way to remove html tags is to use Beautiful Soup, and it takes just one line of code to do this. Pass the string of interest into BeautifulSoup() and use the get_text() method to extract the text without html tags.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">str_cells = str(row_td)
cleantext = BeautifulSoup(str_cells, <span class="hljs-string">"lxml"</span>).get_text()
print(cleantext)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>[14TH, INTEL TEAM M, 04:43:23, 00:58:59 - DANIELLE CASILLAS, 01:02:06 - RAMYA MERUVA, 01:17:06 - PALLAVI J SHINDE, 01:25:11 - NALINI MURARI]
</code></pre><p>Using regular expressions is highly discouraged since it requires several lines of code and one can easily make mistakes. It requires importing the re (for regular expressions) module. The code below shows how to build a regular expression that finds all the characters inside the &lt; td &gt; html tags and replace them with an empty string for each table row.
First, you compile a regular expression by passing a string to match to re.compile(). The dot, star, and question mark (.*?) will match an opening angle bracket followed by anything and followed by a closing angle bracket. It matches text in a non-greedy fashion, that is, it matches the shortest possible string. If you omit the question mark, it will match all the text between the first opening angle bracket and the last closing angle bracket. After compiling a regular expression, you can use the re.sub() method to find all the substrings where the regular expression matches and replace them with an empty string. The full code below generates an empty list, extract text in between html tags for each row, and append it to the assigned list.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> re

list_rows = []
<span class="hljs-keyword">for</span> row <span class="hljs-keyword">in</span> rows:
    cells = row.find_all(<span class="hljs-string">'td'</span>)
    str_cells = str(cells)
    clean = re.compile(<span class="hljs-string">'&lt;.*?&gt;'</span>)
    clean2 = (re.sub(clean, <span class="hljs-string">''</span>,str_cells))
    list_rows.append(clean2)
print(clean2)
type(clean2)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>[14TH, INTEL TEAM M, 04:43:23, 00:58:59 - DANIELLE CASILLAS, 01:02:06 - RAMYA MERUVA, 01:17:06 - PALLAVI J SHINDE, 01:25:11 - NALINI MURARI]





str
</code></pre><p>The next step is to convert the list into a dataframe and get a quick view of the first 10 rows using Pandas.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df = pd.DataFrame(list_rows)
df.head(<span class="hljs-number">10</span>)
</code></pre>
<div>

<h1 id="data-manipulation-and-cleaning">Data Manipulation and Cleaning</h1>
<p>The dataframe is not in the format we want. To clean it up, you should split the "0" column into multiple columns at the comma position. This is accomplished by using the str.split() method.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df1 = df[<span class="hljs-number">0</span>].str.split(<span class="hljs-string">','</span>, expand=<span class="hljs-keyword">True</span>)
df1.head(<span class="hljs-number">10</span>)
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_1.33.19_PM_dwi4fi.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>This looks much better, but there is still work to do. The dataframe has unwanted square brackets surrounding each row. You can use the strip() method to remove the opening square bracket on column "0."</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df1[<span class="hljs-number">0</span>] = df1[<span class="hljs-number">0</span>].str.strip(<span class="hljs-string">'['</span>)
df1.head(<span class="hljs-number">10</span>)
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_1.33.19_PM_dwi4fi.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>The table is missing table headers. You can use the find_all() method to get the table headers.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">col_labels = soup.find_all(<span class="hljs-string">'th'</span>)
</code></pre>
<p>Similar to table rows, you can use Beautiful Soup to extract text in between html tags for table headers.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">all_header = []
col_str = str(col_labels)
cleantext2 = BeautifulSoup(col_str, <span class="hljs-string">"lxml"</span>).get_text()
all_header.append(cleantext2)
print(all_header)
</code></pre>
<pre><code>['[Place, Bib, Name, Gender, City, State, Chip Time, Chip Pace, Gender Place, Age Group, Age Group Place, Time to Start, Gun Time, Team]']
</code></pre><p>You can then convert the list of headers into a pandas dataframe.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df2 = pd.DataFrame(all_header)
df2.head()
</code></pre>
<div>

<p>Similarly, you can split column "0" into multiple columns at the comma position for all rows.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df3 = df2[<span class="hljs-number">0</span>].str.split(<span class="hljs-string">','</span>, expand=<span class="hljs-keyword">True</span>)
df3.head()
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.14.35_PM_i2ogtl.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>The two dataframes can be concatenated into one using the concat() method as illustrated below.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">frames = [df3, df1]

df4 = pd.concat(frames)
df4.head(<span class="hljs-number">10</span>)
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.19.21_PM_xlurna.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>Below shows how to assign the first row to be the table header.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df5 = df4.rename(columns=df4.iloc[<span class="hljs-number">0</span>])
df5.head()
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.22.37_PM_p1gpnm.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>At this point, the table is almost properly formatted. For analysis, you can start by getting an overview of the data as shown below.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df5.info()
df5.shape
</code></pre>
<pre><code>&lt;class 'pandas.core.frame.DataFrame'&gt;
Int64Index: 597 entries, 0 to 595
Data columns (total 14 columns):
[Place              597 non-null object
 Bib                596 non-null object
 Name               593 non-null object
 Gender             593 non-null object

memory usage: 70.0+ KB





(597, 14)
</code></pre><p>The table has 597 rows and 14 columns. You can drop all rows with any missing values.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df6 = df5.dropna(axis=<span class="hljs-number">0</span>, how=<span class="hljs-string">'any'</span>)
</code></pre>
<p>Also, notice how the table header is replicated as the first row in df5. It can be dropped using the following line of code.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df7 = df6.drop(df6.index[<span class="hljs-number">0</span>])
df7.head()
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.25.31_PM_hf5txl.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>You can perform more data cleaning by renaming the '[Place' and ' Team]' columns. Python is very picky about space. Make sure you include space after the quotation mark in ' Team]'.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df7.rename(columns={<span class="hljs-string">'[Place'</span>: <span class="hljs-string">'Place'</span>},inplace=<span class="hljs-keyword">True</span>)
df7.rename(columns={<span class="hljs-string">' Team]'</span>: <span class="hljs-string">'Team'</span>},inplace=<span class="hljs-keyword">True</span>)
df7.head()
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.27.57_PM_ybpuz0.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>The final data cleaning step involves removing the closing bracket for cells in the "Team" column.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df7[<span class="hljs-string">'Team'</span>] = df7[<span class="hljs-string">'Team'</span>].str.strip(<span class="hljs-string">']'</span>)
df7.head()
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.29.29_PM_p137zb.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>It took a while to get here, but at this point, the dataframe is in the desired format. Now you can move on to the exciting part and start plotting the data and computing interesting statistics.</p>
<h1 id="data-analysis-and-visualization">Data Analysis and Visualization</h1>
<p>The first question to answer is, what was the average finish time (in minutes) for the runners? You need to convert the column "Chip Time" into just minutes. One way to do this is to convert the column to a list first for manipulation.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">time_list = df7[<span class="hljs-string">' Chip Time'</span>].tolist()

<span class="hljs-comment"># You can use a for loop to convert 'Chip Time' to minutes</span>

time_mins = []
<span class="hljs-keyword">for</span> i <span class="hljs-keyword">in</span> time_list:
    h, m, s = i.split(<span class="hljs-string">':'</span>)
    math = (int(h) * <span class="hljs-number">3600</span> + int(m) * <span class="hljs-number">60</span> + int(s))/<span class="hljs-number">60</span>
    time_mins.append(math)
<span class="hljs-comment">#print(time_mins)</span>
</code></pre>
<p>The next step is to convert the list back into a dataframe and make a new column ("Runner_mins") for runner chip times expressed in just minutes.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df7[<span class="hljs-string">'Runner_mins'</span>] = time_mins
df7.head()
</code></pre>
<p></p><center><img src="./Web Scraping using Python (article) - DataCamp_files/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.32.52_PM_mgri9u.webp"></center><p></p>
<p>The code below shows how to calculate statistics for numeric columns only in the dataframe.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs">df7.describe(include=[np.number])
</code></pre>

<p>Interestingly, the average chip time for all runners was ~60 mins. The fastest 10K runner finished in 36.35 mins, and the slowest runner finished in 101.30 minutes.</p>
<p>A boxplot is another useful tool to visualize summary statistics (maximum, minimum, medium, first quartile, third quartile, including outliers). Below are data summary statistics for the runners shown in a boxplot. For data visualization, it is convenient to first import parameters from the pylab module that comes with matplotlib and set the same size for all figures to avoid doing it for each figure.</p>
<pre><code class="lang-python hljs"><span class="hljs-keyword">from</span> pylab <span class="hljs-keyword">import</span> rcParams
rcParams[<span class="hljs-string">'figure.figsize'</span>] = <span class="hljs-number">15</span>, <span class="hljs-number">5</span>

</code></pre>

<h1 id="conclusion">Conclusion</h1>
<p>In this tutorial, you performed web scraping using Python. You used the Beautiful Soup library to parse html data and convert it into a form that can be used for analysis. You performed cleaning of the data in Python and created useful plots (box plots, bar plots, and distribution plots) to reveal interesting trends using Python's matplotlib and seaborn libraries. After this tutorial, you should be able to use Python to easily scrape data from the web, apply cleaning techniques and extract useful insights from the data.</p>
<p>If you would like to learn more about Python, take DataCamp's free <a href="https://www.datacamp.com/courses/intro-to-python-for-data-science">Intro to Python for Data Science</a> course.</p>

I do a lot of scraping and prefer to take them one at a time, with the first step downloading the web data in a local database where I can refer to it at anytime for analysis. But connecting to a db is another topic...</p>
<p>I also want to leave a tip about explicitly identifying wanted data in the html. You were find various tags from the entire soup, but most of the time there are multiple tables as well as other tags. Looking at the example html (which, as you point out, the reader can access by right licking and selecting 'inspect') I quickly noticed that the table of interest was assigned an id with a value of 'individualResults'. Reading only this table saves headaches of unwittingly grabbing data from the wrong table. Once the intended table has been isolated the rows and columns can be looped through.&nbsp;</p>
<p>table = soup.find(<strong>'table'</strong>, {'<strong>id'</strong>:'individualResults<strong>'</strong>})</p>
<p>for row in table.findAll('tr'):</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;for col in row.findAll('td'):<br>
</p>
<p>I want to thank you for some Pandas examples. I need to learn more about these packages so I can simplify and expand my analysis.<br>

<p><strong>url = "</strong><a href="https://www.imdb.com/chart/top?ref_=nv_mv_250" target="_blank"><strong>https://www.imdb.com/chart/top?ref_=nv_mv_250</strong></a><strong>"</strong></p>
<p><strong>html = urlopen(url)</strong></p>
<p><strong>url_list = BS(html, 'lxml')</strong></p>
<p><strong>type(url_list)</strong></p>
<p><strong>all_links = url_list.find_all('a', href=re.compile("/title/tt"))</strong></p>
<p><strong>for link in all_links:</strong></p>
<p><strong>&nbsp; print(link.get("href"))</strong></p>
<p><strong>all_urls = link.get("href")</strong></p>

<p><strong>for i in all_urls:</strong></p>

<p>this is the code to scrape the urls of all the 250 movies from the main url.<br>
but the code gives the result as ------</p>

Scraping using Python","slug":"web-scraping-using-python","previewSlug":null,"description":"In this tutorial, you'll learn how to extract data from the web, manipulate and clean data using Python's Pandas library, and data visualize using Python's Matplotlib library.","articles":[],"courses":[],"redirectSlug":null,"contentHtml":"\u003cp\u003eWeb scraping is a term used to describe the use of a program or algorithm to extract and process large amounts of data from the web. Whether you are a data scientist, engineer, or anybody who analyzes large amounts of datasets, the ability to scrape data from the web is a useful skill to have. Let\u0026#39;s say you find data from the web, and there is no direct way to download it, web scraping using Python is a skill you can use to extract the data into a useful form that can be imported.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eIn this tutorial, you will learn about the following:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eâ¢    Data extraction from the web using Python\u0026#39;s Beautiful Soup module\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eâ¢    Data manipulation and cleaning using Python\u0026#39;s Pandas library\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eâ¢    Data visualization using Python\u0026#39;s Matplotlib library\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe dataset used in this tutorial was taken from a 10K race that took place in Hillsboro, OR on June 2017. Specifically, you will analyze the performance of the 10K runners and answer questions such as:\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eâ¢    What was the average finish time for the runners?\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eâ¢    Did the runners\u0026#39; finish times follow a normal distribution?\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eâ¢    Were there any performance differences between males and females of various age groups?\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003ch1 id=\"web-scraping-using-beautiful-soup\"\u003eWeb Scraping using Beautiful Soup\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eUsing Jupyter Notebook, you should start by importing the necessary modules (pandas, numpy, matplotlib.pyplot, seaborn). If you don\u0026#39;t have Jupyter Notebook installed, I recommend installing it using the Anaconda Python distribution which is available on the internet. To easily display the plots, make sure to include the line %matplotlib inline as shown below.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003eimport pandas as pd\r\nimport numpy as np\r\nimport matplotlib.pyplot as plt\r\nimport seaborn as sns\r\n%matplotlib inline\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eTo perform web scraping, you should also import the libraries shown below. The urllib.request module is used to open URLs. The Beautiful Soup package is used to extract data from html files. The Beautiful Soup library\u0026#39;s name is bs4 which stands for Beautiful Soup, version 4.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003efrom urllib.request import urlopen\r\nfrom bs4 import BeautifulSoup\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eAfter importing necessary modules, you should specify the URL containing the dataset and pass it to urlopen() to get the html of the page.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003eurl = \u0026quot;http://www.hubertiming.com/results/2017GPTR10K\u0026quot;\r\nhtml = urlopen(url)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eGetting the html of the page is just the first step. Next step is to create a Beautiful Soup object from the html. This is done by passing the html to the BeautifulSoup() function. The Beautiful Soup package is used to parse the html, that is, take the raw html text and break it into Python objects. The second argument \u0026#39;lxml\u0026#39; is the html parser whose details you do not need to worry about at this point.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003esoup = BeautifulSoup(html, \u0026#39;lxml\u0026#39;)\r\ntype(soup)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ebs4.BeautifulSoup\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe soup object allows you to extract interesting information about the website you\u0026#39;re scraping such as getting the title of the page as shown below.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003e# Get the title\r\ntitle = soup.title\r\nprint(title)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;title\u0026gt;2017 Intel Great Place to Run 10K \\ Urban Clash Games Race Results\u0026lt;/title\u0026gt;\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou can also get the text of the webpage and quickly print it out to check if it is what you expect.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003e# Print out the text\r\ntext = soup.get_text()\r\n#print(soup.text)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eYou can view the html of the webpage by right-clicking anywhere on the webpage and selecting \u0026quot;Inspect.\u0026quot; This is what the result looks like.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531327753/Picture1_w0u783.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eYou can use the find_all() method of soup to extract useful html tags within a webpage. Examples of useful tags include \u0026lt; a \u0026gt; for hyperlinks, \u0026lt; table \u0026gt; for tables, \u0026lt; tr \u0026gt; for table rows, \u0026lt; th \u0026gt; for table headers, and \u0026lt; td \u0026gt; for table cells. The code below shows how to extract all the hyperlinks within the webpage.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003esoup.find_all(\u0026#39;a\u0026#39;)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e[\u0026lt;a class=\u0026quot;btn btn-primary btn-lg\u0026quot; href=\u0026quot;/results/2017GPTR\u0026quot; role=\u0026quot;button\u0026quot;\u0026gt;5K\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;http://hubertiming.com/\u0026quot;\u0026gt;Huber Timing Home\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;#individual\u0026quot;\u0026gt;Individual Results\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;#team\u0026quot;\u0026gt;Team Results\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;mailto:timing@hubertiming.com\u0026quot;\u0026gt;timing@hubertiming.com\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;#tabs-1\u0026quot; style=\u0026quot;font-size: 18px\u0026quot;\u0026gt;Results\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a name=\u0026quot;individual\u0026quot;\u0026gt;\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a name=\u0026quot;team\u0026quot;\u0026gt;\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;http://www.hubertiming.com/\u0026quot;\u0026gt;\u0026lt;img height=\u0026quot;65\u0026quot; src=\u0026quot;/sites/all/themes/hubertiming/images/clockWithFinishSign_small.png\u0026quot; width=\u0026quot;50\u0026quot;/\u0026gt;Huber Timing\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;,\r\n \u0026lt;a href=\u0026quot;http://facebook.com/hubertiming/\u0026quot;\u0026gt;\u0026lt;img src=\u0026quot;/results/FB-f-Logo__blue_50.png\u0026quot;/\u0026gt;\u0026lt;/a\u0026gt;]\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eAs you can see from the output above, html tags sometimes come with attributes such as class, src, etc. These attributes provide additional information about html elements. You can use a for loop and the get(\u0026#39;\u0026quot;href\u0026quot;) method to extract and print out only hyperlinks.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003eall_links = soup.find_all(\u0026quot;a\u0026quot;)\r\nfor link in all_links:\r\n    print(link.get(\u0026quot;href\u0026quot;))\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e/results/2017GPTR\r\nhttp://hubertiming.com/\r\n#individual\r\n#team\r\nmailto:timing@hubertiming.com\r\n#tabs-1\r\nNone\r\nNone\r\nhttp://www.hubertiming.com/\r\nhttp://facebook.com/hubertiming/\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eTo print out table rows only, pass the \u0026#39;tr\u0026#39; argument in soup.find_all().\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003e# Print the first 10 rows for sanity check\r\nrows = soup.find_all(\u0026#39;tr\u0026#39;)\r\nprint(rows[:10])\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e[\u0026lt;tr\u0026gt;\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;Finishers:\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;577\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\u0026lt;/tr\u0026gt;, of 414\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;M 36-45\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;2 of \u0026lt;tr\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;5\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;569\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;DEREK G OSBORNE\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;M\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;HILLSBORO\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;OR\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;00:39:21\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;06:20\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;5 F\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;/tr\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;tr\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;6\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;642\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;JONATHON TRAN\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;M\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;PORTLAND\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;OR\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;00:39:49\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;06:25\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;6 of 414\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;M 18-25\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;1 of 34\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;00:00:06\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;00:39:55\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;\r\n\u0026lt;/tr\u0026gt;]\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe goal of this tutorial is to take a table from a webpage and convert it into a dataframe for easier manipulation using Python. To get there, you should get all table rows in list form first and then convert that list into a dataframe. Below is a for loop that iterates through table rows and prints out the cells of the rows.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003efor row in rows:\r\n    row_td = row.find_all(\u0026#39;td\u0026#39;)\r\nprint(row_td)\r\ntype(row_td)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e[\u0026lt;td\u0026gt;14TH\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;td\u0026gt;INTEL TEAM M\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;td\u0026gt;04:43:23\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;td\u0026gt;00:58:59 - DANIELLE CASILLAS\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;td\u0026gt;01:02:06 - RAMYA MERUVA\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;td\u0026gt;01:17:06 - PALLAVI J SHINDE\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;, \u0026lt;td\u0026gt;01:25:11 - NALINI MURARI\u0026lt;/td\u0026gt;]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nbs4.element.ResultSet\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe output above shows that each row is printed with html tags embedded in each row. This is not what you want. You can use remove the html tags using Beautiful Soup or regular expressions.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe easiest way to remove html tags is to use Beautiful Soup, and it takes just one line of code to do this. Pass the string of interest into BeautifulSoup() and use the get_text() method to extract the text without html tags.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003estr_cells = str(row_td)\r\ncleantext = BeautifulSoup(str_cells, \u0026quot;lxml\u0026quot;).get_text()\r\nprint(cleantext)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e[14TH, INTEL TEAM M, 04:43:23, 00:58:59 - DANIELLE CASILLAS, 01:02:06 - RAMYA MERUVA, 01:17:06 - PALLAVI J SHINDE, 01:25:11 - NALINI MURARI]\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eUsing regular expressions is highly discouraged since it requires several lines of code and one can easily make mistakes. It requires importing the re (for regular expressions) module. The code below shows how to build a regular expression that finds all the characters inside the \u0026lt; td \u0026gt; html tags and replace them with an empty string for each table row.\r\nFirst, you compile a regular expression by passing a string to match to re.compile(). The dot, star, and question mark (.*?) will match an opening angle bracket followed by anything and followed by a closing angle bracket. It matches text in a non-greedy fashion, that is, it matches the shortest possible string. If you omit the question mark, it will match all the text between the first opening angle bracket and the last closing angle bracket. After compiling a regular expression, you can use the re.sub() method to find all the substrings where the regular expression matches and replace them with an empty string. The full code below generates an empty list, extract text in between html tags for each row, and append it to the assigned list.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003eimport re\r\n\r\nlist_rows = []\r\nfor row in rows:\r\n    cells = row.find_all(\u0026#39;td\u0026#39;)\r\n    str_cells = str(cells)\r\n    clean = re.compile(\u0026#39;\u0026lt;.*?\u0026gt;\u0026#39;)\r\n    clean2 = (re.sub(clean, \u0026#39;\u0026#39;,str_cells))\r\n    list_rows.append(clean2)\r\nprint(clean2)\r\ntype(clean2)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e[14TH, INTEL TEAM M, 04:43:23, 00:58:59 - DANIELLE CASILLAS, 01:02:06 - RAMYA MERUVA, 01:17:06 - PALLAVI J SHINDE, 01:25:11 - NALINI MURARI]\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nstr\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe next step is to convert the list into a dataframe and get a quick view of the first 10 rows using Pandas.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf = pd.DataFrame(list_rows)\r\ndf.head(10)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cdiv\u003e\r\n\u003cstyle\u003e\r\n    .dataframe thead tr:only-child th {\r\n        text-align: right;\r\n \u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\u003c/div\u003e\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\u003ch1 id=\"data-manipulation-and-cleaning\"\u003eData Manipulation and Cleaning\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe dataframe is not in the format we want. To clean it up, you should split the \u0026quot;0\u0026quot; column into multiple columns at the comma position. This is accomplished by using the str.split() method.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf1 = df[0].str.split(\u0026#39;,\u0026#39;, expand=True)\r\ndf1.head(10)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531332732/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_1.33.19_PM_dwi4fi.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThis looks much better, but there is still work to do. The dataframe has unwanted square brackets surrounding each row. You can use the strip() method to remove the opening square bracket on column \u0026quot;0.\u0026quot;\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf1[0] = df1[0].str.strip(\u0026#39;[\u0026#39;)\r\ndf1.head(10)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531332732/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_1.33.19_PM_dwi4fi.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe table is missing table headers. You can use the find_all() method to get the table headers.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003ecol_labels = soup.find_all(\u0026#39;th\u0026#39;)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eSimilar to table rows, you can use Beautiful Soup to extract text in between html tags for table headers.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003eall_header = []\r\ncol_str = str(col_labels)\r\ncleantext2 = BeautifulSoup(col_str, \u0026quot;lxml\u0026quot;).get_text()\r\nall_header.append(cleantext2)\r\nprint(all_header)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e[\u0026#39;[Place, Bib, Name, Gender, City, State, Chip Time, Chip Pace, Gender Place, Age Group, Age Group Place, Time to Start, Gun Time, Team]\u0026#39;]\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eYou can then convert the list of headers into a pandas dataframe.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf2 = pd.DataFrame(all_header)\r\ndf2.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cdiv\u003e\r\n\u003cstyle\u003e\r\n    .dataframe thead tr:only-child th {\r\n        text-align: right;\r\n    }\r\n\r\n    .dataframe thead th {\r\n        text-align: left;\r\n    }\r\n\r\n    .dataframe tbody tr th {\r\n        vertical-align: top;\r\n    }\r\n\u003c/style\u003e\r\n\u003ctable border=\"1\" class=\"dataframe\"\u003e\r\n  \u003cthead\u003e\r\n    \u003ctr style=\"text-align: right;\"\u003e\r\n      \u003cth\u003e\u003c/th\u003e\r\n      \u003cth\u003e0\u003c/th\u003e\r\n    \u003c/tr\u003e\r\n  \u003c/thead\u003e\r\n  \u003ctbody\u003e\r\n    \u003ctr\u003e\r\n      \u003cth\u003e0\u003c/th\u003e\r\n      \u003ctd\u003e[Place, Bib, Name, Gender, City, State, Chip T...\u003c/td\u003e\r\n    \u003c/tr\u003e\r\n  \u003c/tbody\u003e\r\n\u003c/table\u003e\r\n\u003c/div\u003e\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\u003cp\u003eSimilarly, you can split column \u0026quot;0\u0026quot; into multiple columns at the comma position for all rows.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf3 = df2[0].str.split(\u0026#39;,\u0026#39;, expand=True)\r\ndf3.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531332957/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.14.35_PM_i2ogtl.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe two dataframes can be concatenated into one using the concat() method as illustrated below.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003eframes = [df3, df1]\r\n\r\ndf4 = pd.concat(frames)\r\ndf4.head(10)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531333284/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.19.21_PM_xlurna.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eBelow shows how to assign the first row to be the table header.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf5 = df4.rename(columns=df4.iloc[0])\r\ndf5.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531333391/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.22.37_PM_p1gpnm.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eAt this point, the table is almost properly formatted. For analysis, you can start by getting an overview of the data as shown below.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf5.info()\r\ndf5.shape\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003e\u0026lt;class \u0026#39;pandas.core.frame.DataFrame\u0026#39;\u0026gt;\r\nInt64Index: 597 entries, 0 to 595\r\nData columns (total 14 columns):\r\n[Place              597 non-null object\r\n Bib                596 non-null object\r\n Name               593 non-null object\r\n Gender             593 non-null object\r\n City               593 non-null object\r\n State              593 non-null object\r\n Chip Time          593 non-null object\r\n Chip Pace          578 non-null object\r\n Gender Place       578 non-null object\r\n Age Group          578 non-null object\r\n Age Group Place    578 non-null object\r\n Time to Start      578 non-null object\r\n Gun Time           578 non-null object\r\n Team]              578 non-null object\r\ndtypes: object(14)\r\nmemory usage: 70.0+ KB\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n(597, 14)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe table has 597 rows and 14 columns. You can drop all rows with any missing values.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf6 = df5.dropna(axis=0, how=\u0026#39;any\u0026#39;)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eAlso, notice how the table header is replicated as the first row in df5. It can be dropped using the following line of code.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf7 = df6.drop(df6.index[0])\r\ndf7.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531333573/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.25.31_PM_hf5txl.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eYou can perform more data cleaning by renaming the \u0026#39;[Place\u0026#39; and \u0026#39; Team]\u0026#39; columns. Python is very picky about space. Make sure you include space after the quotation mark in \u0026#39; Team]\u0026#39;.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf7.rename(columns={\u0026#39;[Place\u0026#39;: \u0026#39;Place\u0026#39;},inplace=True)\r\ndf7.rename(columns={\u0026#39; Team]\u0026#39;: \u0026#39;Team\u0026#39;},inplace=True)\r\ndf7.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531333697/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.27.57_PM_ybpuz0.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe final data cleaning step involves removing the closing bracket for cells in the \u0026quot;Team\u0026quot; column.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf7[\u0026#39;Team\u0026#39;] = df7[\u0026#39;Team\u0026#39;].str.strip(\u0026#39;]\u0026#39;)\r\ndf7.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg src=\"http://res.cloudinary.com/dyd911kmh/image/upload/f_auto,q_auto:best/v1531333818/Screen_Shot_2018-07-11_at_2.29.29_PM_p137zb.png\" /\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eIt took a while to get here, but at this point, the dataframe is in the desired format. Now you can move on to the exciting part and start plotting the data and computing interesting statistics.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003ch1 id=\"data-analysis-and-visualization\"\u003eData Analysis and Visualization\u003c/h1\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe first question to answer is, what was the average finish time (in minutes) for the runners? You need to convert the column \u0026quot;Chip Time\u0026quot; into just minutes. One way to do this is to convert the column to a list first for manipulation.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003etime_list = df7[\u0026#39; Chip Time\u0026#39;].tolist()\r\n\r\n# You can use a for loop to convert \u0026#39;Chip Time\u0026#39; to minutes\r\n\r\ntime_mins = []\r\nfor i in time_list:\r\n    h, m, s = i.split(\u0026#39;:\u0026#39;)\r\n    math = (int(h) * 3600 + int(m) * 60 + int(s))/60\r\n    time_mins.append(math)\r\n#print(time_mins)\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003eThe next step is to convert the list back into a dataframe and make a new column (\u0026quot;Runner_mins\u0026quot;) for runner chip times expressed in just minutes.\u003c/p\u003e\r\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode class=\"lang-python\"\u003edf7[\u0026#39;Runner_mins\u0026#39;] = time_mins\r\ndf7.head()\r\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\r\n\u003cp\u003e\u003ccenter\u003e\u003cimg
